<div id="review_150478648" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/150478648" />
    <a title="Connie  Kuntz" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1664750-connie-kuntz"><img alt="Connie  Kuntz" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1463878527p2/1664750.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/150478648?book_show_action=true">Feb 25, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Connie  Kuntz" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Connie  Kuntz" href="/user/show/1664750-connie-kuntz">Connie  Kuntz</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/10219794-twenty-three-things-they-don-t-tell-you-about-capitalism">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer150478648" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11801817431451003578">This book is a very convincing argument about how utterly bogus the notions of free market and free trade actually are. You can find a quote in every chapter regarding this point. My favorite is found on page 73: <br/><br/>"The free trade, free-market policies that have rarely, if ever, worked. Most of the rich countries did not uses such policies when they were developing countries themselves, while these policies have slowed down growth and increased income inequality in the developing countries in the </span>
  <span id="freeText11801817431451003578" style="display:none">This book is a very convincing argument about how utterly bogus the notions of free market and free trade actually are. You can find a quote in every chapter regarding this point. My favorite is found on page 73: <br /><br />"The free trade, free-market policies that have rarely, if ever, worked. Most of the rich countries did not uses such policies when they were developing countries themselves, while these policies have slowed down growth and increased income inequality in the developing countries in the last three decades. Few countries have become rich through free-trade, free-market policies and few ever will." <br /><br />Ha-Joon Chang wrote 23 chapters ("things") about common misconceptions about U.S. economics. I highly recommend reading this book, but if you are pressed for time, I offer my following synopsis entitled "The Pooping Triangle."<br /><br />Picture a triangle. One point is Immigration. The other point is National Identity. The other point is Government. Immigration needs National Identity to exist so it can exist. National Identity needs the Government to exist so it can exist. Immigration needs the Government to exist so it can exist, and so on... <br /><br />The triangle I am describing is a political triangle that essentially feeds off itself. When the triangle eats too much of itself and has to take a poop, it gives said poop to the poor. The poop is difficult to identify because the triangle gave it a good spin. They spun it very fast. So fast that the poop became so unidentifiable, the poor asked the rich, "What is that?" The rich then answered, "That is the American Dream. That is The Free Market. That is...Your American Duty." <br /><br />If you think that was lame, I offer you the following quote from Donald Rumsfeld found on page 174-175:<br /><br />"There are known knowns. There are things we know that we know. There are known unknowns. That is to say, there are things that we now know we don't know. But there are also unknown unknowns. There are things we do not know we don't know." <br /><br />I stand by my pooping triangle, Mr. Rumsfeld!<br /><br />Moving on, my point is this: it is no secret that the people who run the corporations who are so anti-immigration are the same people who locate their business anywhere (internationally, not just in the U.S.) as long as they profit, in spite of the fact that they are stealing land and opportunity from the natives. It is also no secret that the people who fund the corporations who behave this way are in the government. These superpowers control the majority of the money.<br /><br />And about our money. There is a very funny chapter about the "dead presidents" on our money.<br /><br />age 68:<br />"The dead presidents don't talk. But if they could, they would tell Americans and the rest of the world how the policies that their successors promote today are the exact opposite of what they used in order to transform a second-rate agrarian economy dependent on slave labour into the world's greatest industrial power." <br /><br />And what of those "good old days" anyway? Was there ever such a thing?<br /><br />Before you think I am a total ungrateful jerk, I offer the following paragraph:<br /><br />Do we have opportunity in the United States. Absolutely. Do I love my country? 100%. Am I angry about my life? Not at all. Reading about all of this "big stuff" helps me understand my small life better. <br /><br />While the book considers national identity, ultimately, I am more concerned about my family identity. While the book considers the U.S.'s closest political ally, I find myself thinking about my closest political ally. My husband. While the book also considers Child Labor laws, I find myself thinking about my own childrens' chores. Yes, it is a no-brainer that children shouldn't work by adult standards, but the following quote struck me in a special way.<br /><br />"The same reason why we send our children to school rather than making them compete with adults in the labour market, developing countries need to protect and nurture their producers before they acquire the capabilities to compete in the world market unassisted."<br /><br />When I read that (and other passages about child labour laws) it dawned on me that I need to nurture my kids as kids more, rather than constantly prep them for the adult world. <br /><br />This is a funny, intelligent book. Chang has a way of getting the reader to consider un-patriotic concepts in a humorous, intelligent way. He concludes the book very optimistically and I will defintiely read his other book "Bad Samaritans" in the future. <br /><br />Read this book if you enjoy economics, sociology, current political agendas and raising a family.<br />I recommend that you have your dictionary handy. That is, have it handy unless you can easily and quickly define words such as agrarian, sobriquet, suasion and many others. <br /><br />I also spent a good minute wondering how and when "cotton" became a verb. (I like it as a verb, by the way.)<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11801817431451003578" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_555899425" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/555899425" />
    <a title="Athan Tolis" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/18185192-athan-tolis"><img alt="Athan Tolis" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1483618484p2/18185192.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/555899425?book_show_action=true">Mar 08, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Athan Tolis" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Athan Tolis" href="/user/show/18185192-athan-tolis">Athan Tolis</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18185192-athan-tolis?shelf=politics">politics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18185192-athan-tolis?shelf=ec-and-finance">ec-and-finance</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer555899425" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16489578087085429733">The book is full of information, and it's written very clearly. That's a plus, because you can use your spare mental capacity to process this information properly while the author is busy making a terrible hash of it.<br/><br/>I'll be upfront here and admit I come from very similar leanings as the author. But that's precisely why I'm angry with the book. How can somebody so educated and so concerned about the issues allocate the time to write a book and come up so short?<br/><br/>One extremely annoying thing about </span>
  <span id="freeText16489578087085429733" style="display:none">The book is full of information, and it's written very clearly. That's a plus, because you can use your spare mental capacity to process this information properly while the author is busy making a terrible hash of it.<br /><br />I'll be upfront here and admit I come from very similar leanings as the author. But that's precisely why I'm angry with the book. How can somebody so educated and so concerned about the issues allocate the time to write a book and come up so short?<br /><br />One extremely annoying thing about the book is that the author is so consumed with "debunking myths" that he totally misses the forest for the trees.<br /><br />Like, he has an entire chapter where he's so consumed with debunking the link between education and income (a common excuse in America for why the poor are in trouble) that he forgets where his average reader is coming from. I, for one, am quite happy that more people are going to college. If they got tricked into doing so thinking it's some sort of ticket to prospertiy, hey, whatever works!<br /><br />Or he has another chapter where he rehashes the questionable argument that the washing machine is a bigger invention than the computer. This is a building block toward later saying we don't live in the post-industrial age and you should not have to go to college, I guess. But the truth is it's way way way too early to judge. As I'm writing this, my wife is at home working from her computer. Yeah, maybe she has the washing machine on. But my shirts are at the cleaners'. Jury's out, basically, and the credibility of all people in my camp goes down with the rest of this book.<br /><br />Or he says capital is not international, it's national. Well, we now all know the truth is ten times more nuanced. GM designs its small cars in South Korea, for example, but if it ever goes to die again, it will die again in America. Capital always DIES at home (or asks for help at home). Yes, the arguments the author makes can also be valid. But for goodness' sake, you are destroying their credibility if you don't present the other side.<br /><br />The second thing I don't like about the book is the author needs to man up.<br /><br />For example, he goes telling us the US is doing things poorly, maintains a third world country within it (all of it true stuff, and I wholeheartedly agree) but then when he goes to pick the opposite, the prime example of how thigs are meant to be done, it turns out he likes, get ready for this... Norway. Or Finland. News flash: Norway is rich for the same reason Kuwait is rich. Not because they run the system you and I like. And let's talk about Finland in a couple years' time when no ten-year-old knows what a Nokia phone is. If you really want to build the case for the prosecution, there is no way round explaining FRANCE. The government there is bigger than 50% of GDP, they do loads of planning, they graduate people from the ENA and the other Grandes Ecoles, they are self-sufficient in power because they went nuclear, it should be your paradise. Man up and tell us why you like France. Or Germany. I don't care. But spare us the Scandies. They all fit in my living room.<br /><br />Or he goes telling us that the industrial jobs are just as important as they used to be. A lie. Yes, we use twice as many things as we used to, but we have the way to make them with less input from labor. It's just how it is. If the author had the courage, he'd admit that there are people who for better or for worse will never be able to become "ideas people" like engineers or doctors or paper pushers and who are also not suited to the services industry and we can either pay for them from the dole or we can pay for them to go to work. Germany understands this so Germany subsidizes their employers. That's how the author needs to build the argument. Or, alternatively, follow the line of argument which states that you cannot expect to design well if you put distance between design and production. Not give us the mumbo jumbo about how we are still an industrial society.<br /><br />THE most annoying thing in this book, however, and by a country mile, is that for 90% of the arguments the author uses the same proof: So-and-so country (dunno, the US, the UK, all of Africa) was on some massive path to prosperity right after the Great Depression and the Second World War and in the past 30 years things are no longer so hot. So it must be because we put taxes down on the guys who pay them (i.e. the rich) Well, let me tell you something, my Freshman Year in college I missed 17 crew practices due to mononucleosis, and my progress when I got back was truly enormous, but then I reached some type of plateau. You need to do better.<br /><br />And you can do better, if you go into the nitty-gritty of the arguments. But it takes courage to say you like France because its marcroeconomic numbers currently reflect that the "automatic stabilizers" are working overtime. It takes courage to say that Apple / Foxconn might lose to Samsung because you could get the principle right and the example wrong. It takes courage to risk losing your reader. And, most importantly, it takes courage to concede points to the other side.<br /><br />So I was very disappointed by this book. It's a laundry list and nothing more. And by lacking honesty and proof for its arguments, it's a failure.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16489578087085429733" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3387855664" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8004441183273440554">Finally a book that says it like it is and decimates the neoliberalistic lies we've been touted with one by one. Written in a way that is easily understandable and readable regardless of your earlier insights into economics and the so-called free-market. Learn about how there is no such thing as a free market (thing 1), how companies should not be run in the interest of their owners (thing 2) and 21 other things that will make you understand the world we live in better. Deeply insightful and abs</span>
  <span id="freeText8004441183273440554" style="display:none">Finally a book that says it like it is and decimates the neoliberalistic lies we've been touted with one by one. Written in a way that is easily understandable and readable regardless of your earlier insights into economics and the so-called free-market. Learn about how there is no such thing as a free market (thing 1), how companies should not be run in the interest of their owners (thing 2) and 21 other things that will make you understand the world we live in better. Deeply insightful and absolutely lacerating. Should be mandatory reading for politicians. I'm starting to wonder whether they are illiterate, ignorant or simply don't care. It's probably the last.</span>

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review3387855664">
  <td colspan="2">

<div id="review_158931917" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/158931917" />
    <a title="Ob-jonny" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/180214-ob-jonny"><img alt="Ob-jonny" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1291334856p2/180214.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/158931917?book_show_action=true">Apr 05, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ob-jonny" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ob-jonny" href="/user/show/180214-ob-jonny">Ob-jonny</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer158931917" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12122190887563240961">This is a great book on economics, the free market, and trade policy. Everything is explained in a very understandable way without getting the reader lost in too many details or obscure terms that only an economics expert would understand. It dispels a lot of myths about how the free-market is good for the economy of the country where it is practiced. It describes in detail about how the free-market ideology has destroyed or stagnated the economies of countries in sub-Saharan Africa and Latin Am</span>
  <span id="freeText12122190887563240961" style="display:none">This is a great book on economics, the free market, and trade policy. Everything is explained in a very understandable way without getting the reader lost in too many details or obscure terms that only an economics expert would understand. It dispels a lot of myths about how the free-market is good for the economy of the country where it is practiced. It describes in detail about how the free-market ideology has destroyed or stagnated the economies of countries in sub-Saharan Africa and Latin America. It describes how well South Korea did with protectionist policies and how the young United States in the 1800s grew even though it had some of the highest tariffs in the world. Another really interesting explanation is the real meaning of per-capita income, median income, and purchasing power. The United States is about 6th in per-capita income excluding small nations, but this is so high because the rich are so rich. The median income is down lower in rank compared to other countries and the median income describes much better how the average person is doing. Also, the purchasing power is more relevant than per-capita income because different countries have different cost of services independent on average income. The ultimate statistic about how well a country is doing is the purchasing power of the median income person in that country.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12122190887563240961" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_145575188" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/145575188" />
    <a title="Christopher Rex" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2064068-christopher-rex"><img alt="Christopher Rex" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1291012326p2/2064068.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/145575188?book_show_action=true">Feb 03, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Christopher Rex" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Christopher Rex" href="/user/show/2064068-christopher-rex">Christopher Rex</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/10219794-twenty-three-things-they-don-t-tell-you-about-capitalism">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer145575188" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1710546275200518626">This book would make an excellent companion to any economics class. It is better as a "discussion starter" and/or "debate" type of hypothesis than a standard economical study. That within itself is the book's greatest strength and weakness. Many of the arguments put forth here are not backed up w/ methodical statistical studies and data - that's obviously a good thing for the "casual" reader, like myself. The problem is, that without widespread and detailed case-studies it opens itself up to cri</span>
  <span id="freeText1710546275200518626" style="display:none">This book would make an excellent companion to any economics class. It is better as a "discussion starter" and/or "debate" type of hypothesis than a standard economical study. That within itself is the book's greatest strength and weakness. Many of the arguments put forth here are not backed up w/ methodical statistical studies and data - that's obviously a good thing for the "casual" reader, like myself. The problem is, that without widespread and detailed case-studies it opens itself up to criticism. Regardless, it is important to point out that the author is NOT a "socialist", he is an admitted "capitalist" but feels that the current system dominated by the US is fatally flawed in many ways. In other words, the theories behind "free market capitalism" are simply incorrect on a lot of issues, and therefore in need of reform. It is a very interesting read which challenges many of the basic tenets of capitalism that we are all taught to believe, whether we study the subject or not. In that right, it is well-written and interesting for the economist, student of economics and "dude on the street" as well. Solid effort.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1710546275200518626" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_160803989" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/160803989" />
    <a title="Sarah" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1845103-sarah"><img alt="Sarah" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1279933200p2/1845103.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/160803989?book_show_action=true">Apr 13, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sarah" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sarah" href="/user/show/1845103-sarah">Sarah</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer160803989" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9928400059162802538">I consider this a must-read for anyone like me, who does not have an economics background and has become accustomed to the misleading free market rhetoric. It was refreshing to have some of the principles of my own thoughts on the economy reinforced by a prize-winning economist, as well as to hear other more complicated principles developed by Ha-Joon Chang that challenge the current orthodoxy. The book is incredibly straightforward and easy to read, in a conversational voice I would almost call</span>
  <span id="freeText9928400059162802538" style="display:none">I consider this a must-read for anyone like me, who does not have an economics background and has become accustomed to the misleading free market rhetoric. It was refreshing to have some of the principles of my own thoughts on the economy reinforced by a prize-winning economist, as well as to hear other more complicated principles developed by Ha-Joon Chang that challenge the current orthodoxy. The book is incredibly straightforward and easy to read, in a conversational voice I would almost call breezy and fun. No understanding of economics is necessarily, as he explains any concepts that are necessary to understand his points. Chang does not miss an opportunity to point out the hypocrisy and wrongheadedness of certain practices in the supposed free market brand of capitalism, to comic effect. I do plan to read some critiques of his perspective, but this book was well-supported with statistical evidence and did not feel like mere political polemic. </span>
  <a data-text-id="9928400059162802538" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/28781546-ana?shelf=east-asian-literature">east-asian-literature</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1094687319" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17342436969257254056">“Recognizing that the boundaries of the market are ambiguous and cannot be determined in an objective way lets us realize that economics is not a science like physics or chemistry, but a political exercise... If the boundaries of what you are studying cannot be scientifically determined, what you are doing is not a science.” <br/><br/>....Mind=blown...</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_211092231" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/211092231" />
    <a title="Adam Higgitt" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6367795-adam-higgitt"><img alt="Adam Higgitt" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1345502343p2/6367795.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/211092231?book_show_action=true">Sep 19, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Adam Higgitt" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Adam Higgitt" href="/user/show/6367795-adam-higgitt">Adam Higgitt</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer211092231" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11288874469050331988">This is a book that could only have been published in 2010. A confluence between the style of Freakonomics and an appetite for tracts like The Spirit Level it may be, but the timing of Ha-Joo Chang’s book is important for another reason. It is a reappraisal of economic orthodoxy only possible once appropriate distance has been gained from the manifest failure of that orthodoxy.<br/><br/>23 Things The Don’t Tell You About Capitalism takes the sacred cows of free market economics one-by-one and calmly slaug</span>
  <span id="freeText11288874469050331988" style="display:none">This is a book that could only have been published in 2010. A confluence between the style of Freakonomics and an appetite for tracts like The Spirit Level it may be, but the timing of Ha-Joo Chang’s book is important for another reason. It is a reappraisal of economic orthodoxy only possible once appropriate distance has been gained from the manifest failure of that orthodoxy.<br /><br />23 Things The Don’t Tell You About Capitalism takes the sacred cows of free market economics one-by-one and calmly slaughters them. It is not a phillipic against capitalism per se – Chang makes clear that he believes our economic system to be the best we can hope for. Instead, 23 Things The Don’t Tell You About Capitalism builds over a sequence of rebuttals an argument for the return of features of older capitalist orders long since discredited: interventionist government industrial policy, the importance of manufacturing, stronger regulation of financial markets and the detachment of corporate interest from the broader public interest. And he points out the persistent fallacies and myths of free market capitalism: how we still live in a planned economy, how there is no international free market in wages, how a rising tide does not lift all boats and how the information revolution is over-hyped.<br /><br />There are straw men here (has any serious person ever suggested that Africa is “destined for underdevelopment”?) and the chapter on equality of opportunity is horribly confused. The targets of Chang’s ire may occasionally feel justified in demanding he do more than cite anecdote in his counter-arguments, but overall this a welcome corrective, and another part in the jigsaw that will surely in time help bring about the reappraisal he seeks.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11288874469050331988" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1538128191" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1538128191" />
    <a title="Victoriano" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/46519541-victoriano"><img alt="Victoriano" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442049979p2/46519541.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1538128191?book_show_action=true">Feb 05, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Victoriano" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Victoriano" href="/user/show/46519541-victoriano">Victoriano</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/46519541-victoriano?shelf=nonfiction">nonfiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1538128191" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer852253970443549730">If there’s one thing an average 20 year-old steeped in Western culture has heard about, it’s capitalism. It seems to be casually referenced in a lot of films, and television shows, and intellectual conversations in college dorms over your third ramen dinner of the week. Any self-respecting adult knows a thing or two about economics, and Ha-Joon Chang definitely capitalizes (pun intended) on that concept. So, for the people out there who want a cursory glance at the development of capitalism in 3</span>
  <span id="freeText852253970443549730" style="display:none">If there’s one thing an average 20 year-old steeped in Western culture has heard about, it’s capitalism. It seems to be casually referenced in a lot of films, and television shows, and intellectual conversations in college dorms over your third ramen dinner of the week. Any self-respecting adult knows a thing or two about economics, and Ha-Joon Chang definitely capitalizes (pun intended) on that concept. So, for the people out there who want a cursory glance at the development of capitalism in 300 pages, 23 Things They Don’t Tell You about Capitalism is the book for you.<br /><br />I know that title suggests that something conspiratorial is afoot, but really the entire crux of this book is that capitalist thought permeates through every aspect of society. People just choose to ignore it, and thus passively accepts the concepts of free-market capitalism. To be honest, you will probably realize that you’ve considered most of the ideas in this book at least once in your life, which is sort of the point. What Chang does is give you the vocabulary and the statistics to back up the obvious.<br /><br />This book makes excellent use of the interdisciplinary nature of economics: every one of the 23 points is inextricably linked to politics, anthropology and group psychology. So obviously there’s something in this for you, and your family, and your boss, and that girl in the boho dress you met at a rally once. I also really appreciate the level of restraint that Chang employs when expressing dissenting opinions over free-market capitalism. All he does is lay down some facts, and you get to do the rest. You get to decide what to focus on, and what you want to do with it. And really, isn’t that what capitalism is all about?</span>
  <a data-text-id="852253970443549730" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer14921643740686065381">If you're a proponent of free-market economy, this book would challenge you to reconsider your position or the very least to look more critically at the flaws of free-market economy in practice across the globe. Prior to reading this book, I was exposed primarily to free-market economy literature e.g., writings of Friedrich Hayek, Milton Friendman, Thomas Sowell, Walter Williams and Adam Smith. IIn my humble assessment, overall free-market economy had served humanity well; but the world is chang</span>
  <span id="freeText14921643740686065381" style="display:none">If you're a proponent of free-market economy, this book would challenge you to reconsider your position or the very least to look more critically at the flaws of free-market economy in practice across the globe. Prior to reading this book, I was exposed primarily to free-market economy literature e.g., writings of Friedrich Hayek, Milton Friendman, Thomas Sowell, Walter Williams and Adam Smith. IIn my humble assessment, overall free-market economy had served humanity well; but the world is changing, hard to ignore the widening gap between the rich and poor, social inequality, income disparity and such. Ha-Joon Chang invite his readers to the argument that free-market economy is not the answer to the domestic, regional and global challenges of today's. I think perhaps what we need is a mixed-market economy. I don't even know what's that really entails--I'm still learning but reading this book certainly put things into perspective. <br />I agree with the author that "capitalism is the worst economic system except for all the others." p. 253</span>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_187498106" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/187498106" />
    <a title="Mat" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5856248-mat"><img alt="Mat" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1408287797p2/5856248.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/187498106?book_show_action=true">Jul 22, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mat" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mat" href="/user/show/5856248-mat">Mat</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781846143281" href="/book/show/9136467-23-things-they-don-t-tell-you-about-capitalism">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer187498106" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5504297742595192742">In this critically-acclaimed book, economic iconoclast Ha-Joon Chang makes a mockery of the beliefs of modern-day capitalists.<br/><br/>Here, in a nutshell, are the 23 points made by the author, who has won the Wassily Leontief Prize for Advancing the Frontiers of Economic Thought.<br/><br/>1. There is no such thing as a free market.<br/><br/>There are always some rules, and they are set by politics, not the market. Wages are set by government through immigration. Child labour and pollution laws were once contested by capita</span>
  <span id="freeText5504297742595192742" style="display:none">In this critically-acclaimed book, economic iconoclast Ha-Joon Chang makes a mockery of the beliefs of modern-day capitalists.<br /><br />Here, in a nutshell, are the 23 points made by the author, who has won the Wassily Leontief Prize for Advancing the Frontiers of Economic Thought.<br /><br />1. There is no such thing as a free market.<br /><br />There are always some rules, and they are set by politics, not the market. Wages are set by government through immigration. Child labour and pollution laws were once contested by capitalists, but they are now widely accepted. Free marketeers say they are scientific, but they are political.<br /><br />2. Companies should not be run in the interests of their owners.<br /><br />Shareholders can easily and quickly move their money elsewhere, so they are interested in only short-term profits. Such profits come through cutting overheads such as staff, workers’ rights and pay, none of which benefit companies or economies in the long run.<br /><br />3. Most people in rich countries are paid more than they should be.<br /><br />They are often less skilled than their counterparts in poor countries, but wages are determined by immigration. Free marketeers complain about unions and the minimum wage, but - strangely - not immigration. True free marketeers would let immigrants in.<br /><br />4. The washing machine has changed the world more than the internet has.<br /><br />The washing machine freed up much time and labour, but studies have struggled to find how the internet has changed productivity. Free marketeers fetishise the internet because they say it has brought down international borders, so workers need to be more flexible. Cross-border flows of capital, labour and goods come from politics, not technology.<br /><br />5. Assume the worst about people and you get the worst.<br /><br />Free marketeers believe the market works because everyone is self-seeking. But if that were true, the market would grind to a halt, since everyone would be trying to cheat each other. Companies have to trust their workers - if they spent all their time distrusting and checking staff, their profits would dive.<br /><br />6. Greater macroeconomic stability has not made the world economy more stable.<br /><br />Much has been made of inflation being tamed, but the world economy has become considerably shakier, with a huge number of financial crises and far more job insecurity. Under low inflation, income growth has fallen and the world economy has slowed.<br /><br />7. Free-market policies rarely make poor countries rich.<br /><br />Developing countries grew better under state-led development than free markets. The UK, US and their ilk became rich through protectionism and subsidies, not free markets. In rejecting British pressure to adopt free trade, US president Ulysses Grant said: “Within 200 years, when America has gotten out of protection all that it can offer, it too will adopt free trade.” It has not, yet.<br /><br />8. Capital has a nationality.<br /><br />Much is made of globalisation, but transnational companies remain national companies with international operations. It is hard to move many aspects of a company across borders, including human capital. Firms usually buy only existing firms overseas, rather than creating new ones.<br /><br />9. We do not live in a post-industrial age.<br /><br />As manufacturing productivity has increased, the value of products has decreased in comparison with services. This has led to the false assumption that rich countries now have service economies, since the service sector is worth more. An emphasis on services slows productivity and thus slows economies. It is a fantasy to say developing countries can skip straight to a service economy.<br /><br />10. The US does not have the highest standard of living in the world.<br /><br />US inequality is high, so the average wage is skewed. A dollar in the US can buy more services, but only because labour is cheap due to immigration and low wages. Seven countries had higher per capita income than the US in 2007. Americans also have far less leisure time, so their quality of life is lower and they work more for less pay.<br /><br />11. Africa is not destined for underdevelopment.<br /><br />Africa posted decent growth in the 1960s and 70s. Its so-called physical handicaps such as extreme weather and ethnic divisions are all experienced by rich countries. Free-market policies are the real handicap and policies can be changed.<br /><br />12. Governments can pick winners.<br /><br />South Korea made its steel industry a success against all odds and forced the company LG to go into electronics and Hyundai into shipbuilding. Computers, the internet and biotechnology would not have been developed to the same extent without extensive US government investment. If only businesses knew best, none would make a bad decision, but just look at Microsoft and its disastrous Windows Vista operating system. Saying only the private sector picks winners would ignore half the world’s successes.<br /><br />13. Making rich people richer doesn’t make the rest of us richer.<br /><br />Progressive taxation from 1950 to 1973 brought the highest growth rates in most of the rich capitalist countries, known as “The Golden Age of Capitalism“. With tax cuts for the rich, investment as a rate of national output has fallen. The best way out of a downturn is to give money to the poor, because they spend it.<br /><br />14. US managers are over-priced.<br /><br />They are paid 10 times more than their more successful predecessors in the 1960s and 20 times more than their more successful contemporaries in other countries. To justify their pay, they must increase short-term profits by damaging their companies’ long-term prospects, eventually requiring taxpayer-funded bailouts. Therefore, CEO pay should not be determined by the market.<br /><br />15. People in poor countries are more entrepreneurial than people in rich countries.<br /><br />They have to be to survive. In rich countries, most people work for companies. In poor countries, half or more of the non-agricultural workforce is self-employed - 87.7% in Benin, but only 7.5% in the US. Poor countries have individual entrepreneurs, but rich countries get rich through collective entrepreneurship, supported by infrastructure, legislation, education and co-operation between companies.<br /><br />16. We are not smart enough to leave things to the market.<br /><br />Even Nobel prize-winning economists and top bankers do not truly understand complex financial instruments. Instruments that are too complex for experts to understand should be banned, like dangerous products.<br /><br />17. More education in itself is not going to make a country richer.<br /><br />The east Asian “miracle economies” had lower literacy rates than less successful economies. From 1980 to 2004, literacy rates in sub-Saharan Africa rose, but per capita income fell. Karl Marx said capitalists deliberately de-skill the workforce to make them more sackable and to increase production through mechanisation.<br /><br />18. What is good for General Motors is not necessarily good for the United States.<br /><br />It is best to restrict the freedom of firms so they do not destroy human and natural resources. Instead of making better cars, GM called for protectionism and turned itself into a finance company, eventually requiring a $US57 billion bailout.<br /><br />19. Despite the fall of communism, we are still living in planned economies.<br /><br />Capitalist governments pay for a significant share of research and development, infrastructure, and plan a large chunk of the economy through state-owned enterprises. Capitalists expect CEOs to have detailed business plans, so they should not expect governments to be dictated to by the whims of the market.<br /><br />20. Equality of opportunity may not be fair.<br /><br />Even if, say, education is open to all, some children will still be disadvantaged through malnutrition. It’s like a race where everyone has a head start but some people run with weights on their legs. Equality of outcome can only be achieved through a welfare state.<br /><br />21. Big government makes people more open to change.<br /><br />If people have welfare and retraining to fall back on, they will be more open to change in their work, making for a more dynamic economy.<br /><br />22. Financial markets need to become less, not more, efficient.<br /><br />Holders of financial assets can move their money around too quickly, leading to economic instability. The move to financial assets means long-term investments have declined, leading to slower growth.<br /><br />23. Good economic policy does not require good economists.<br /><br />The most successful economic bureaucrats are usually not economists - they were lawyers in Japan and Korea and engineers in Taiwan and China. Economists are harmful for economies and people. Their influence culminated in the 2008 financial crisis. We have far more to learn from the likes of Marx than from free-market economists.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5504297742595192742" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2075894843" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2075894843" />
    <a title="Kevin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/35434974-kevin"><img alt="Kevin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1571256914p2/35434974.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2075894843?book_show_action=true">Jul 29, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kevin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kevin" href="/user/show/35434974-kevin">Kevin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/35434974-kevin?shelf=econ-general">econ-general</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/35434974-kevin?shelf=econ-development">econ-development</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2075894843" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10606038809004632444">3rd book I read by Chang, a reformist State Capitalist tearing down Free Market fundamentalism. Given his experiences with South Korea's development, he is a worthy State Capitalist to engage with.<br/><br/>Before we go any further, the most accessible real-world econ 101 book is, imho: <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/36490332.Talking_to_My_Daughter_About_the_Economy_or__How_Capitalism_Works___and_How_It_Fails' title='Talking to My Daughter About the Economy or, How Capitalism Works - and How It Fails by Yanis Varoufakis' rel='nofollow noopener'>Talking to My Daughter About the Economy: or, How Capitalism Works - and How It Fails</a><br/><br/>OK, this book:<br/><b>*The Good</b>:<br/>--Some reviewers criticize the execution for Issue #1 (There is no Free Market). I agree, especially because late</span>
  <span id="freeText10606038809004632444" style="display:none">3rd book I read by Chang, a reformist State Capitalist tearing down Free Market fundamentalism. Given his experiences with South Korea's development, he is a worthy State Capitalist to engage with.<br /><br />Before we go any further, the most accessible real-world econ 101 book is, imho: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/36490332.Talking_to_My_Daughter_About_the_Economy_or__How_Capitalism_Works___and_How_It_Fails" title="Talking to My Daughter About the Economy or, How Capitalism Works - and How It Fails by Yanis Varoufakis" rel="nofollow noopener">Talking to My Daughter About the Economy: or, How Capitalism Works - and How It Fails</a><br /><br />OK, this book:<br /><b>*The Good</b>:<br />--Some reviewers criticize the execution for Issue #1 (There is no Free Market). I agree, especially because later Chang mentions a much more illustrative criticism of Market fundamentalism, when he introduces <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/89879.Herbert_A__Simon" title="Herbert A. Simon" rel="nofollow noopener">Herbert A. Simon</a>. <br />--Simon visualizes the economy as having large portions of public (Government) bureaucracies, private (corporate) bureaucracies, and networks, connected with (relatively smaller) market relations. This contrasts from the market fundamentalist illustration of an economy of rational self-interested individuals connected by market relations. <br />--Chang's examples of capitalist reforms are interesting, particular his emphasis on prioritizing production (improving: machinery, infrastructure, labor training); still, he seems at his best trashing Free Market fundamentalists in the context of development economics, which his book <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1032019.Bad_Samaritans_The_Myth_of_Free_Trade_and_the_Secret_History_of_Capitalism" title="Bad Samaritans The Myth of Free Trade and the Secret History of Capitalism by Ha-Joon Chang" rel="nofollow noopener">Bad Samaritans: The Myth of Free Trade and the Secret History of Capitalism</a> already covers. <br />--Introduces the speed gap between finance (the most liquid) and industry (requires long-term investment)...<br />--Introduces our limited rationality, and the dangers of complexity (esp. overly-complex financial instruments); the fact that transparency here is not enough, we do not need more information as we are limited in processing such information, we need to ban such instruments of mass destruction...<br /><br /><b>*The Bad</b>:<br /><i>Free Market economists are just useful idiots.</i><br />Seriously, why play their game? The elites behind "Neoliberalism" knew well enough that they were engaged in short-term pillage, a class war. We need to examine <b>power</b>, and quite frankly an academic State Capitalist like Chang has little critical input here. Consider:<br />-<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/194805.Understanding_Power_The_Indispensable_Chomsky" title="Understanding Power The Indispensable Chomsky by Noam Chomsky" rel="nofollow noopener">Understanding Power: The Indispensable Chomsky</a><br />-<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/13330433.The_Democracy_Project_A_History__a_Crisis__a_Movement" title="The Democracy Project A History, a Crisis, a Movement by David Graeber" rel="nofollow noopener">The Democracy Project: A History, a Crisis, a Movement</a><br /><br /><i>Is profit the crisis?</i><br />Another topic missing: the <b>crisis of falling rates of profits</b> from race-to-the-bottom competition and overproduction. While I agree Financial Capitalism is the most volatile, what is Industrial Capitalism's relationship to boom/bust cycles? Was not the Golden Age of Capitalism fueled by the complete destruction of capital all over the world (from the ravages of WWII), thus temporarily recovering profitability? As Japanese and European manufacturing was rebuilt and caught up to American manufacturing, did this increased competition not dive down profitability? Was "Neoliberalism" not a political move to Financial Capitalism <i>because</i> of such declining profits (as opposed to causing the decline)?<br />Consider: <br />-<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/11415678.The_Global_Minotaur_America__the_True_Origins_of_the_Financial_Crisis_and_the_Future_of_the_World_Economy" title="The Global Minotaur America, the True Origins of the Financial Crisis and the Future of the World Economy by Yanis Varoufakis" rel="nofollow noopener">The Global Minotaur: America, the True Origins of the Financial Crisis and the Future of the World Economy</a><br />-<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/322903.The_Economics_of_Global_Turbulence_The_Advanced_Capitalist_Economies_from_Long_Boom_to_Long_Downturn__1945_2005" title="The Economics of Global Turbulence The Advanced Capitalist Economies from Long Boom to Long Downturn, 1945-2005 by Robert Brenner" rel="nofollow noopener">The Economics of Global Turbulence: The Advanced Capitalist Economies from Long Boom to Long Downturn, 1945-2005</a><br /><br /><i>The paradox of State Capitalist development.</i><br />Is the crisis of falling rates of profits only a concern for developed countries? Put in another way, does rapid development require capitalism and its exploding inequities? State Capitalism seems like a house of cards. Chang scratches the surface when comparing productivity and wages between rich and poor countries, suggesting the crucial role of immigration control to preserve higher wages in rich countries. What a strange paradox: for rich Capitalist countries to maintain tolerable labor conditions, they must keep other countries noncompetitive (underdeveloped, i.e. poor) and crush market forces with unequal trade policies, labor movement restrictions (immigration control), massive subsidies to domestic industries, outright military/economic terrorism, etc. etc.<br /> <br /> <i>Orwell was a Socialist.</i><br />Seriously, why is Socialism always the USSR? What happened to <b>Socialism as worker self-management</b>? How limited we leave ourselves with social policy, with Chang recommending we need to preserve Capitalism and just have more Government. Chang should realize that since there is no Free Market, neoliberalism is just an international bureaucracy, an international Government to prop up an international market that destroys all alternatives. <br /><br />...I challenge Chang to address the following, which would dismantle Capitalism (profit, private ownership of production/distribution/exchange) as we know it:<br />1) Public banking<br />2) Worker co-operatives, owned-and-operated. This also ties in with automation. Coordinated through federations (decentralized).</span>
  <a data-text-id="10606038809004632444" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_201629187" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/201629187" />
    <a title="Ushan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4696922-ushan"><img alt="Ushan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1451676557p2/4696922.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/201629187?book_show_action=true">Aug 23, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ushan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ushan" href="/user/show/4696922-ushan">Ushan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781846143281" href="/book/show/9136467-23-things-they-don-t-tell-you-about-capitalism">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4696922-ushan?shelf=economics">economics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer201629187" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2568612689275007277">In the 1960s, South Korea, then a military dictatorship, wanted to be self-sufficient in steel. It founded a steel company with the government as the major shareholder, an ocean away from the nearest deposits of iron ore and coking coal (there was then no trade between Communist China and anti-Communist South Korea), headed by a retired general who knew nothing about steel, using Japanese reparation payments as capital. This project was thought of by most investors as insane. The company is now </span>
  <span id="freeText2568612689275007277" style="display:none">In the 1960s, South Korea, then a military dictatorship, wanted to be self-sufficient in steel. It founded a steel company with the government as the major shareholder, an ocean away from the nearest deposits of iron ore and coking coal (there was then no trade between Communist China and anti-Communist South Korea), headed by a retired general who knew nothing about steel, using Japanese reparation payments as capital. This project was thought of by most investors as insane. The company is now the world's third-largest steel maker by market value. Chang's book consists of 23 short essays, each on a thing about capitalism that is usually thought to be false, but is in fact true. One of the 23 things is that governments can pick winners. Now, at around the same time newly independent Ghana also built many state-owned factories, which failed spectacularly. Sometimes governments can pick winners, and sometimes they cannot. Another of the 23 things that Chang says are true is that free-market policies rarely make poor countries richer. Surely, India is a poor country that became much richer since it became less protectionist and more free-market starting in 1991, and China since 1979! What is the combined population of the poor countries that failed to become richer, and how does it compare to that of India plus that of China? Most of the remaining 21 things look reasonable to me, and are interesting to think about. US managers are indeed over-priced: they run their companies no better than managers in other rich countries despite commanding much higher salaries. Reading that big government makes people more open to change reminded me of a conversation with a Norwegian resident who said that in Norway, programmers are very willing to join startups: if the startup succeeds, they will profit, and if it fails, the welfare state will make it so their lives won't change significantly for the worse.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2568612689275007277" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_399772342" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/399772342" />
    <a title="Answer" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3449214-answer"><img alt="Answer" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1388758831p2/3449214.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/399772342?book_show_action=true">Aug 25, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Answer" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Answer" href="/user/show/3449214-answer">Answer</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780141047973" href="/book/show/10733801-23-things-they-don-t-tell-you-about-capitalism">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3449214-answer?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3449214-answer?shelf=capitalism">capitalism</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3449214-answer?shelf=economic-justice">economic-justice</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer399772342" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18235624692205375845">"23 Things They Don't Tell you About Capitalism" is basically a 'pop' version of Ha-Joon Chang's previous work, Bad Samaritans. This book seems to be intended to be very easy to read -which is good to some extent- but unfortunately it has made the discussion a bit too oversimplified. <br/><br/>Ha-Joon Chang starts every chapter with a brief explanation on 'myths' he claimed put forward by the capitalists and free market advocates. I, however, found that some of the 'myths' he mentions in the book are not </span>
  <span id="freeText18235624692205375845" style="display:none">"23 Things They Don't Tell you About Capitalism" is basically a 'pop' version of Ha-Joon Chang's previous work, Bad Samaritans. This book seems to be intended to be very easy to read -which is good to some extent- but unfortunately it has made the discussion a bit too oversimplified. <br /><br />Ha-Joon Chang starts every chapter with a brief explanation on 'myths' he claimed put forward by the capitalists and free market advocates. I, however, found that some of the 'myths' he mentions in the book are not really arguments used by the capitalists to defend their ideology and belief on free market. The 'myth' in Thing 11 on 'Africa is not destined for under development', for instance, is an argument which I think likely to be given by a racist, but not a modern capitalist :-/<br /><br />I also found Chang's explanation on Thing 3 re 'Most People in Rich Countries are Paid More than They Should Be' wasn't plausible. Chang claimed that the capitalist will say it's OK to pay a Swedish bus driver 50 times more than an Indian bus driver because the former works 50 times more efficient than the latter one. I am aware the capitalists are all for efficient works and valuing hard works as well as talents (often excessively) but I don't think a capitalist would say it's because of his efficient works that the Swedish bus driver is paid a lot more. I think free market defenders would just say that the Swedish chap is paid more because living costs in Sweden are somewhat higher than in India - and this is surely what Chang should have countered? :-/<br /><br />But overall, I see this book as a very good effort of Chang to popularise the discussion on capitalism and make it attractive for young people. I also like that he raises the issue of inequality and justice in this book. When I read some parts of it, I felt for a second that I was reading a book on contextualised moral philosophy instead of one on capitalism.</span>
  <a data-text-id="18235624692205375845" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <a title="Johnsergeant" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Johnsergeant" href="/user/show/220391-johnsergeant">Johnsergeant</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audiobook " href="/book/show/13437202-twenty-three-things-they-don-t-tell-you-about-capitalism">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/220391-johnsergeant?shelf=economics">economics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/220391-johnsergeant?shelf=current-affairs">current-affairs</a>,

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/220391-johnsergeant?shelf=audiblecom">audiblecom</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer240910926" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5345284467749369470">One of the best critiques of free market capitalism I have read!<br/><br/>Narrated by Joe Barrett<br/><br/>8 hrs and 58 mins<br/><br/>Publisher's Summary<br/><br/>Thing 1: There is no such thing as the free market.<br/>Thing 4: The washing machine has changed the world more than the Internet.<br/>Thing 5: Assume the worst about people, and you get the worst.<br/>Thing 13: Making rich people richer doesn't make the rest of us richer.<br/><br/>If you've wondered how we did not see the economic collapse coming, Ha-Joon Chang knows the answer: We didn't ask what </span>
  <span id="freeText5345284467749369470" style="display:none">One of the best critiques of free market capitalism I have read!<br /><br />Narrated by Joe Barrett<br /><br />8 hrs and 58 mins<br /><br />Publisher's Summary<br /><br />Thing 1: There is no such thing as the free market.<br />Thing 4: The washing machine has changed the world more than the Internet.<br />Thing 5: Assume the worst about people, and you get the worst.<br />Thing 13: Making rich people richer doesn't make the rest of us richer.<br /><br />If you've wondered how we did not see the economic collapse coming, Ha-Joon Chang knows the answer: We didn't ask what they didn't tell us about capitalism. This is a lighthearted book with a serious purpose: to question the assumptions behind the dogma and sheer hype that the dominant school of neoliberal economists - the apostles of the freemarket - have spun since the Age of Reagan.<br />Chang, the author of the international best seller Bad Samaritans, is one of the world's most respected economists, a voice of sanity - and wit - in the tradition of John Kenneth Galbraith and Joseph Stiglitz.<br /><br />23 Things They Don't Tell You About Capitalism equips listeners with an understanding of how global capitalism works - and doesn't. In his final chapter, "How to Rebuild the World", Chang offers a vision of how we can shape capitalism to humane ends, instead of becoming slaves of the market.<br />Ha-Joon Chang teaches in the Faculty of Economics at the University of Cambridge. His books include the best-selling Bad Samaritans: The Myth of Free Trade and the Secret History of Capitalism. His Kicking Away the Ladder received the 2003 Myrdal Prize, and, in 2005, Chang was awarded the Leontief Prize for Advancing the Frontiers of Economic Thought.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="5345284467749369470" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_3095634782" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3095634782" />
    <a title="Ryan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1500718-ryan"><img alt="Ryan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1238846167p2/1500718.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3095634782?book_show_action=true">Dec 22, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ryan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ryan" href="/user/show/1500718-ryan">Ryan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3095634782" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6205416163634603402">In <i>23 Things They Don't Tell You About Capitalism</i>, Ha-Joon Chang explains the shortcomings of free-market capitalism. Because HJC is poking holes in the broad stories neoliberals tell us, there is a risk of enjoying or disliking this book because of motivated reasoning. But many of these essays are built on easily understood scenarios that are fun to think about. Why, for example, might a bus driver in Switzerland makes 50 times more than a bus driver from India?<br/><br/>The conventional (free market) wi</span>
  <span id="freeText6205416163634603402" style="display:none">In <i>23 Things They Don't Tell You About Capitalism</i>, Ha-Joon Chang explains the shortcomings of free-market capitalism. Because HJC is poking holes in the broad stories neoliberals tell us, there is a risk of enjoying or disliking this book because of motivated reasoning. But many of these essays are built on easily understood scenarios that are fun to think about. Why, for example, might a bus driver in Switzerland makes 50 times more than a bus driver from India?<br /><br />The conventional (free market) wisdom is that the Swiss driver is more skilled and delivers more value. At first glance, however, the Indian driver probably navigates trickier highways so is more skilled, and how much more value can any bus driver really deliver relative to another? Globally, the markets are not free and do not reward merit.<br /><br />Instead, they’re protected by borders, so HJC consistently describes borders as a sort of trade protectionism. He then expands on this scenario to wonder why the bus driver in India makes so much less. It’s not because the driver cannot compete; in a free market, the Indian driver would be very competitive. It is therefore because the Swiss elites deliver their nation much much more value than their Indian counterparts. So maybe we should stop complaining about the one percenters—they deserve their wealth, right?<br /><br />But Chang next argues that this elite productivity advantage is actually possible only because of systems and institutions. Warren Buffet could not become a billionaire in most countries, regardless of how patiently and insightfully he invests. Although I’m not sure anything is too big to fail, it does seem much harder to fail, and much easier to succeed, if one is born into wealth within a society with strong institutions. (I often wondered if historic advantages are very expensive to overcome. By this I mean that improving living standards might be easier to solve than overcoming inherited advantage.)<br /><br />After reading this chapter on the bus drivers, I went for a walk. As I looked at the different houses, some nicer than others and others more ostentatious than their neighbours, I thought about conclusions people might reach based on the bus driver analysis.<blockquote>-“Chang is wrong. The system works.”<br />-“Chang is right about the system's flaws, but the system works for me.” <br />-“Chang is right that people mostly don’t deserve their lot, but I do because I worked hard/ smartly.”<br />-“Chang is right: the rich don’t deserve all of their riches.”<br />-“Chang is wrong because those poorer than I deserve their lack of riches but he is right because people richer than me do not deserve their riches.”<br />-“Chang is right: those poorer than I don’t deserve their lack of riches, but I’ll give up neither mine nor hope others earn more than I have/ stand to.”<br />-“Borders are important to me, but for unselfish reasons. People from other cultures do not deserve to be here.”<br />-“The world is not fair; therefore borders are important to me for selfish but not unethical within a fundamentally unethical system.”<br />-“Chang is right, but people don’t want to think it even if they intuitively feel it. Therefore, if I declare that borders are important, people will agree because they understand the value of border protection. All I have to do is rationalize that borders are important.”<br />-“Chang is right, but people don’t want to believe all of the implications. Therefore, I can construct a populist message that the wealthy do not deserve their wealth but it will be important to not think more globally.”<br />-“Because people mostly do not deserve their riches, we should aim to reduce material inequality.”<br />-“As much as possible, we should try to give as many people as possible a reasonable standard of living. If we have to lower the ceiling to raise the floor, fine.”<br />-“As much as possible, we should try to empower the elite. If we have to lower the floor to raise the ceiling, fine.”</blockquote> What I found most interesting was that I could talk myself into a variety of these thoughts either consciously or unconsciously. Or maybe the right way to think about these ideas is that few, perhaps none, apply universally. At the end of the day, however, I kept coming back to a strong feeling that people want to be generous but not at the expense of their own advantage. In other words, no one wants to move from, say, middle class to lower class in order to raise a class floor.<br /><br />Some other random notes. On education: Chang argues that people mostly overestimate the connection between education and the economy. He argues that the economy relies on education to sort out diligent and bright people, a claim that educators tend to dislike. He also points out that measuring the standard of living in the USA is very difficult because Americans are willing to accept so much poverty and incarceration relative to other nations. I wonder if the reason Americans so often think their country enjoys a high standard of living is because they excel at thinking that their unsuccessful peers are in some way not people or are somehow lesser people--not peers in other words. The final chapter is perhaps the most hilarious. In it, Chang argues that a strong economy rarely if ever has anything to do with economists. Reading this chapter, it occurred to me not for the first time that economists excel at promoting their discipline relative to psychologists, physicists, historians... Somehow, for a discipline so often committed to self-interest, and one notorious for its very direct criticisms of each other's work, economists as a group seem to have solved the prisoner's dilemma when it comes to group promotion.<br /><br /><i>23 Things They Don't Tell You About Capitalism</i> was written just after the financial collapse, but I was surprised by how well it holds up in 2019. It's not difficult to read this book and connect it many stories today.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6205416163634603402" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
